  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_0dbaa6d6-8b02-430f-8eca-8fa0f06da214'

<span id="freeTextContainer18274202685282118219">Noah York is a closeted gay teenager with a foul mouth, a critical disposition, and plenty of material for his tirades. After his father dies, Noah's mother, a temperamental poet, takes a teaching job in a small New Hampshire town, far from Chicago and the only world Noah has known. While Noah gets along reasonably with his mother, the crumbling house they try to renovate </span>
  <span id="freeText18274202685282118219" style="display:none">Noah York is a closeted gay teenager with a foul mouth, a critical disposition, and plenty of material for his tirades. After his father dies, Noah's mother, a temperamental poet, takes a teaching job in a small New Hampshire town, far from Chicago and the only world Noah has known. While Noah gets along reasonably with his mother, the crumbling house they try to renovate quickly reveals dark secrets, via dusty Mason jars they discover interred between walls. The jars contain scraps of letters, poems, and journal entries, and eventually reconstructs a history of pain and violence that drives a sudden wedge between Noah and his mother. Fortunately, Noah finds an unexpected ally in J.D., a teenager down the street who has family troubles of his own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18274202685282118219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/163802-help-just-finished-reading-leave-myself">help just finished reading Leave Myself Behind and when I hit about page 204 with the chapter ending at a big yellow moving van outside the house, and then jumped back to page 158 and repeated from there.

Would someone please tell me how it ends, from the moving van arriving onward. Please.....</a>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/16621526-beth-cottrell"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1598963565i/16621526._UX100_CR0,38,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/16621526-beth-cottrell">Beth Cottrell</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    You find out that the baby got dropped and that was how it died, the mother then starved herself to death. J.D's parents move away and his mother is s<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    You find out that the baby got dropped and that was how it died, the mother then starved herself to death. J.D's parents move away and his mother is still a bitch, but Tom tells Noah more about what exactly happened with her rape to make her like that. The letter that explained everything that happened to the baby and Nellie that they found in the wall of Noah's mum's room he takes to his mum and she gets out of hospital. There's a hint that she may start a relationship with Walter Danvers, her colleague. He tells Noah that she's always talking about him which is nice. And the end is a bit weak, but Noah ends it with thinking about how he doesn't know what's going to happen with him and J.D but how he loves him. They toss the baby's bones in the river. You also find out, Noah's middle name is Allen and J.D's initials stand for Jason David...... <br /><br />Don't know if you still needed this comment, but there you go! <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

<div id="review_21379677" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21379677" />
    <a title="Ami" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/134600-ami"><img alt="Ami" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485783876p2/134600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21379677?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ami" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ami" href="/user/show/134600-ami">Ami</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/134600-ami?shelf=lgbtq-fiction">lgbtq-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/134600-ami?shelf=5-stars_rating">5-stars_rating</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21379677" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1381892865283386704">It is rather unfortunate that this book is categorized as "Gay Fiction" because for those who are not into gay stories will lose the chance to discover one of the most entertaining teenager characters since Holden Caulfield in "<i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>" as well as one of the most endearing relationships ever portrayed in a book. <br/><br/>"<i>Leave Myself Behind</i>" is a truly profound and unforgettable coming-out story of Noah York, who discovered his first love with J.D. Curtis, the boy down the street. I guess</span>
  <span id="freeText1381892865283386704" style="display:none">It is rather unfortunate that this book is categorized as "Gay Fiction" because for those who are not into gay stories will lose the chance to discover one of the most entertaining teenager characters since Holden Caulfield in "<i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>" as well as one of the most endearing relationships ever portrayed in a book. <br /><br />"<i>Leave Myself Behind</i>" is a truly profound and unforgettable coming-out story of Noah York, who discovered his first love with J.D. Curtis, the boy down the street. I guess the gem of this book lies on the character of Noah. He's intelligent, brutally honest, sarcastic, hilarious, and thoroughly lovable. The way he descibes loneliness, his relationship with his Mom, and his thoughts of other small things in daily life is engaging. <br /><br />Then of course, the development of his relationship with J.D. will make you believe in love and hope and happy ending future. That one scene where Noah sits in his porch and sees J.D. walking towards him - the description of the reactions of what happened when the lovers' eyes met, is just breathtaking. Simply one of the best and most entertaining novels I have ever read - gay or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1381892865283386704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_203300219" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203300219" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203300219?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203300219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5880982342534817865">First published in 2004, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> may have appealed to me if I had grown up in a time with fewer queer books. It features a mostly functional gay relationship that I imagine in 2004 would have been groundbreaking in a lot of ways. So, I want to honor how this book’s representation of gay teenagers may have very well helped many gay teens upon its initial publication over a decade ago, when things for queer youth were even worse than they are now. I also appreciated the consistent them</span>
  <span id="freeText5880982342534817865" style="display:none">First published in 2004, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> may have appealed to me if I had grown up in a time with fewer queer books. It features a mostly functional gay relationship that I imagine in 2004 would have been groundbreaking in a lot of ways. So, I want to honor how this book’s representation of gay teenagers may have very well helped many gay teens upon its initial publication over a decade ago, when things for queer youth were even worse than they are now. I also appreciated the consistent theme of how our parents affect us, as well as how our parents are affected by events that can so often escape our awareness. <br /><br />I feel like <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> took on a few too many topics though. There’s the recognition of gayness within the two male love interests, their coming out, an assault motivated by homophobia, our main character’s father recently died, our main character’s mother is obsessed with tearing down the house she and her son live in, our main character’s boyfriend’s mother has a bunch of drama going on, and more. I felt that instead of addressing a few of these topics in-depth, Bart Yates touched on each of them to keep the plot moving instead of providing more thorough exploration of these events and their emotional impact. The many different forces at play in this novel gave it the potential for lots of poignant nuance, yet the overload of topics itself prevented the book from reaching that full potential.<br /><br />I also really did not like Noah, our main character. Yes, I support unlikable main characters, though I tend to support unlikable main characters when their unlikability is rooted in how we may unfairly judge them for traits that activate our stereotypes (e.g., an outspoken female character who is perceived as cold or annoying). However, with Noah, I sensed that he lacked much depth. Walking away from the book, I felt like I did not know much about him, other than that he likes art and occasionally makes fatphobic and sexist comments. Perhaps providing more internal reflection on his part that focused on his emotional reactions to all the events going on around him would have deepened his character. While he had some interesting philosophical musings, they sometimes would veer into problematic territories (e.g., when he thinks that the potential drawbacks of consensual sex are roughly equivalent to the potential drawbacks of sexual assault).<br /><br />Overall, an okay book if you want a more retro queer YA read. However, I would much rather recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/494453741?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i>
</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291486295?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1#comment_form" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Miseducation of Cameron Post</i>
</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1296714862?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>More Happy Than Not</i>
</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51741480?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>What They Always Tell Us</i>
</a>. Still searching for my next fab queer YA read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5880982342534817865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_116321515" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116321515" />
    <a title="Tristan MacAvery" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3095778-tristan-macavery"><img alt="Tristan MacAvery" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1262372181p2/3095778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116321515?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristan MacAvery" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristan MacAvery" href="/user/show/3095778-tristan-macavery">Tristan MacAvery</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer116321515&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116321515" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6872870738040232986">After reading all the hype on the cover, I was ready for this book to be nothing short of brilliant. A gay coming-of-age novel, with a high school senior narrator (Noah) falling for a junior (J.D.), published in the early part of the new millennium -- surely, this will contain new insights and reactions, new ways of discovering this anciently modern accursed blessing called sexuality and self? Alas, despite some critics comparing the book to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4121438.Salinger_J_D_' title='Salinger J.D.' rel='nofollow noopener'>Salinger J.D.</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8340838.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye' title='The Catcher in the Rye by Salinger Jerome David' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Catcher in the Rye</a>, I was sadly di</span>
  <span id="freeText6872870738040232986" style="display:none">After reading all the hype on the cover, I was ready for this book to be nothing short of brilliant. A gay coming-of-age novel, with a high school senior narrator (Noah) falling for a junior (J.D.), published in the early part of the new millennium -- surely, this will contain new insights and reactions, new ways of discovering this anciently modern accursed blessing called sexuality and self? Alas, despite some critics comparing the book to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4121438.Salinger_J_D_" title="Salinger J.D." rel="nofollow noopener">Salinger J.D.</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8340838.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye by Salinger Jerome David" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a>, I was sadly disappointed.<br /><br />The first 80 or so pages was comparatively cliche, as we build up to the obligatory "first disastrous sexual experience moment," and another 30 pages to the equally obligatory "I was wrong, Noah, I hate the girl, I really want you" moment. Out of 244 pages, this is a big chunk of build-up. By page 141, we get the "Oh my God we're found out" cliche, followed by the "Getting beaten up by the high school jocks" cliche a dozen or so pages later. A not quite as cliche happening has Noah's lover moving into his (Noah's) home and J.D.'s family literally moving out of town and abandoning the boy. The ending, not quite "happily ever after" but comparatively upbeat for all that, is vaguely anti-climactic, not to mention vaguely unbelievable.<br /><br />The vast majority of this book is not so much about Noah and J.D.'s romance as it is of Noah's belief that anything good has to be paid for, quite possibly with blood. Noah's father has long since passed, died of a heart attack in his study, found later by young Noah. His mother is a borderline psychotic (made so, we discover, by having been raped by her own father), who tears up the house in which they live to look for clues about the previous owner. Said previous owner was also crazy, and ended up killing his wife and the child she carried that wasn't his. J.D.'s mother, we later find, was raped as well, and J.D. is the result, which explains why she hates him so much, and why J.D.'s father is a cowardly drunkard who allows his wife to beat the boy on a regular basis.<br /><br />How many rape/murder/incest cliches can we dump into one story? The various rages, obsessions, breakdowns, shattering of families... it's far too much for anyone to follow, much less dare to deal with. Generally, they don't deal with it -- J.D.'s family simply disappears, leaving the boy with "that pervert" (oh, is that the part we're supposed to be focusing on?). Noah's mother eventually gets some psychiatric help, and we assume she's going to get better. She's the only one really accepting of the boys' relationship; in fact, she knew long before Noah would even admit it to himself. So the person who accepts him is psychotic. As Noah keeps pointing out, there's a price to pay.<br /><br />Oh -- one other cliche: J.D.'s ex-girlfriend gets to confront him, to say that it's Noah's fault for leading him into this perversion, and her preacher says that he can help J.D. come back to God, otherwise J.D. is going to burn in hell. One quick jab at the ex-gay ministry (which, I thoroughly agree, needs as much jabbing as we can give it).<br /><br />It's no picnic being in high school. Anyone who says how much fun they had in their high school years is delusional, outright lying, or was one of those "popular" ones who, thirty years later, is running his own Amway business and hasn't had an original thought in his life. Add into this vitriolic mix discovering that one is gay, and you've got quite a horrible time of it, even (especially?) now.<br /><br />What seems to be missing in this angst-ridden novel of the horrors of being a teen is some sort of (for lack of a better term) focus. So much happens outside of Noah's exploration and reconciliation of himself that it seems that the book isn't so much about him as it's about how being gay is nothing compared with everyone else's rape, incest, murder, and general psychosis. <br /><br />I wish that author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/45900.Bart_Yates" title="Bart Yates" rel="nofollow noopener">Bart Yates</a> had decided to tell a story about a boy and his lover, rather than adding in so much other drama and madness that it obscures nearly every aspect of the relationship between the (presumably) primary characters. Tell a love story, or tell a story of crazy people who have one comparatively normal person who falls in love. This book seems to be screaming, "There's nothing wrong with me -- hell, I'm just gay, and everyone else is criminally insane. See? Being gay isn't nearly as bad as all this, even though I have to take you, Constant Reader, through all this sewage in order to pound that point into your head."<br /><br />I think I'll go back to reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/150238.Patricia_Nell_Warren" title="Patricia Nell Warren" rel="nofollow noopener">Patricia Nell Warren</a>; her classic works such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/343741.The_Front_Runner" title="The Front Runner by Patricia Nell Warren" rel="nofollow noopener">The Front Runner</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1130921.Fancy_Dancer" title="Fancy Dancer by Patricia Nell Warren" rel="nofollow noopener">Fancy Dancer</a> are much more compelling stories of coming to terms with one's sexuality, as well as coming to find a true lover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6872870738040232986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_200538703" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200538703" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6087599-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530478892p2/6087599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200538703?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/6087599-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6087599-laura?shelf=5-star-club">5-star-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6087599-laura?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200538703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11950462450924802101">I just finished reading this one for the second time (for book club) and while it’s a wonderful book, I can admit objectively that it isn’t perfect, isn’t the best or most brilliant of the novels I would list in my personal “top 15,” but <i>damn</i>, I absolutely LOVE this book. I really do. <br/><br/>And sacrilege that it may be, while I get the comparisons with <i>Catcher in the Rye</i> (Noah as a “Gay Holden Caulfield” and all that jazz) I have to admit that I like <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> much more than C.I.T.R, and lov</span>
  <span id="freeText11950462450924802101" style="display:none">I just finished reading this one for the second time (for book club) and while it’s a wonderful book, I can admit objectively that it isn’t perfect, isn’t the best or most brilliant of the novels I would list in my personal “top 15,” but <i>damn</i>, I absolutely LOVE this book. I really do. <br /><br />And sacrilege that it may be, while I get the comparisons with <i>Catcher in the Rye</i> (Noah as a “Gay Holden Caulfield” and all that jazz) I have to admit that I like <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> much more than C.I.T.R, and love Noah (who in my opinion is funnier, sweeter, and a hell of a lot more self-aware) way more than Holden. I love all three of the main characters, actually, like Noah both Virginia and J.D. are flawed but wonderful, incredibly nuanced individuals. <br /><br />Beyond the characters, and the witty, dry humor which is so much fun to read, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> has some extremely wise, powerful things to say about human beings, the complex and often twisted worlds we create for ourselves and others, and how impossible it is to truly escape the past. Despite some darker, very deep themes, what really sets the plot and themes and characters apart is the honest but never maudlin vein of tenderness, linking it all together so beautifully.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11950462450924802101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2131609994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2131609994" />
    <a title="Cristina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10105913-cristina"><img alt="Cristina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545001055p2/10105913.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2131609994?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristina" href="/user/show/10105913-cristina">Cristina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10105913-cristina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10105913-cristina?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10105913-cristina?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2131609994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1567265649097467912">I've read Bart Yates' debut novel <i> Leave Myself Behind </i> after reading - and adoring - his second book, <i> The Brothers Bishop </i> and I've found it very compelling, with a great narrative voice and full of those elements and issues that are further developed in his follow-up release. <br/><br/>Noah York - a sarcastic, smart 17-year-old - moves with his mother into an old house after relocating to the small town of Oakland following the sudden death of his father.<br/><br/>During the restoration works, Noah and Virginia </span>
  <span id="freeText1567265649097467912" style="display:none">I've read Bart Yates' debut novel <i> Leave Myself Behind </i> after reading - and adoring - his second book, <i> The Brothers Bishop </i> and I've found it very compelling, with a great narrative voice and full of those elements and issues that are further developed in his follow-up release. <br /><br />Noah York - a sarcastic, smart 17-year-old - moves with his mother into an old house after relocating to the small town of Oakland following the sudden death of his father.<br /><br />During the restoration works, Noah and Virginia start finding jars hidden in the walls containing poems, pictures and other items belonging to the house's previous owners. The mystery surrounding these findings will break the precarious balance reached in the life of Noah and his mother.<br /><br />Their story is further complicated by another crucial event: Noah falling in mutual love with his neighbour J.D., a circumstance that triggers a series of dramatic consequences in the lives of both young men.<br /><br />As in Yates' second novel, family dynamics and momentous places (here the house, there the cottage and its back garden) weave together to provide the reader with a web of connections, long-buried stories, moments of tenderness and confrontations that reflect upon the ideas of family, love and trust.<br /><br />Noah's narrative voice is a very compelling one: at times too smart for his own good, full of the black and white distinctions typical of teenagers, he goes through his and J.D.'s family tragedies with a sense of growing awareness and desperation but also holding on to the notion of love - for his mother and J.D. - as at least one fixed certainty in a series of devastating disasters. Noah is not naive or simplistic in his expectations but he does recognise the strength of the bond that connects him with his difficult mother and with the shy but steadfast J.D.<br /><br /><i> Leave Myself Behind </i> is also a novel full of ghosts and remembrances of things past: Noah's father, Virginia's relationship with her own dad, J.D.'s mother's past and, above all, the lurking presence of Nellie Mitchell and Stephen Carlisle (the previous owners of the house) fill the pages of the novel with echoes of the past and with a clear sense that prior events never really cease to exercise their power or cast a shadow onto the present.<br /><br />Perhaps, this novel is not as tightly-knit, gut-wrenching and blindingly powerful as <i> The Brothers Bishop </i> but it's really involving and persuasive, with lots of wonderful passages and with a set of beautiful characters.<br /><br />Whichever way you want to go about it, Bart Yates is a writer deserving of great praise and attention.<br /><br />Highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1567265649097467912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_749726141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/749726141" />
    <a title="Rata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25069340-rata"><img alt="Rata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382820759p2/25069340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/749726141?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rata" href="/user/show/25069340-rata">Rata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer749726141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8929702758588770490">I really had a hard time rating this book. Why? Because on one hand, Bart Yates did a really great job concerning characters and narrative tone (see below), but on the other, the plot has what I perceived here as a major flaw: too much drama.<br/><br/>Okay, so first to the things I really liked about the book:<br/><br/>1. The protagonist, Noah. Noah can be a real asshole, but, (and that's what makes him so likeable), he not only <i>knows</i> he's a pain in the ass at times, he <i>resents</i> it on various occasions: he has a loo</span>
  <span id="freeText8929702758588770490" style="display:none">I really had a hard time rating this book. Why? Because on one hand, Bart Yates did a really great job concerning characters and narrative tone (see below), but on the other, the plot has what I perceived here as a major flaw: too much drama.<br /><br />Okay, so first to the things I really liked about the book:<br /><br />1. The protagonist, Noah. Noah can be a real asshole, but, (and that's what makes him so likeable), he not only <i>knows</i> he's a pain in the ass at times, he <i>resents</i> it on various occasions: he has a loose tongue, an inclination to cynism, and there are situations he feels bad about both. It’s like his mouth runs ahead of his brain about half the time, and he just can’t help it. <br />I like Noah so much because he feels like a full-fledged, believable, three dimensional teenage boy instead of some cliché. He's not just snippy and rude. He's also very insightful and reflective (e.g. when it comes to his extraordinary mom who writes snobby poetry or his recently deceased dad whom he misses a great deal, or the relationship his parents had). He has a good understanding of love, wants to be loved, and loves back deeply. Apart from that, his narrating is hilarious. Refreshing. He tells this story--using lots of swearing--and his words don't get boring for a second.<br /><br />2. Noah's mom. Right on the first page, Noah tells us: "Living with Virginia is like living with a myth. She's only half-human; the rest is equal parts wolverine, hyena, goddess and rutting goat. In other words: she's a poet." <br />Virginia is a great, powerful female character, in my opinion. She's successful, she knows what she wants, she gets shit done. She has a ton of admirable character traits, but she has as many flaws. As the story unfolds, we see her at her most vulnerable: after her husband died, she and Noah moved from Chicago to a rural town in New England where she got a job at the local college and also a Victorian house both are busy to renovate. Behind wood paneling, hidden in the walls, the floors, they find jars the previous (now dead) inhabitants hid there: a mysterious woman--a poet like Virginia--and her insufferable husband. It soon becomes clear something awful must have happened in the old house, and the more messages they find, the more Virgina gets frantic, haunted, tears whole walls down in search for some meaning Noah can't begin to understand. His mother comes apart at the seams for (to him) unknown reasons, and her transformation from a well-organized, headstrong individual to a broken down (still headstrong) mess is unsettling. Her dynamic with Noah is great.<br /><br />3. Noah's budding romantic relationship with J.D., a neighbor boy who tries very hard not to be gay and is burdened with a mother who seems to hate him. <br />J.D.'s a nice contrast to Noah's foul mouth, and their story as well as Noah's reflections about it is believable and well told. There are several at times mildly explicit sex scenes, delivered by Noah's narration in an offhand manner. I liked those especially. Basically, I liked Noah's comfortableness with his sexuality. Well done.<br /><br />So, what's not to like?<br /><br />There is too much going on in this book. <br />There is a reason Virgina reacts the way she does. There is a reason J.D.'s mom treats him as bad as she does. It isn't hard to guess both have in common something bad that happened in their past. Of course, Noah and J.D. have to suffer for it in the present. So. This alone would have been enough source for drama (for 244 pages, that is), but then there is also the drama unfolding when Noah's and J.D.'s relationship becomes known (rural town, remember).<br />While Virginia's problems feel well enough treated by showing the after-effects, the queer shaming (and worse) feels like something the author deemed necessary. As in: this problem isn't carried out with the depth I wished for. It feels half-baked because there isn't enough time in the narration for it to develop, so that subplot seems a bit loose (e.g., the boys are absent from school for a long time—which has the effect they can deal with their family issues, like, the first-order-problems. So, why bother with the school-subplot at all? Why not just make J.D.’s homosexuality known to his family alone and concentrate on the fallout there?)<br />Now to J.D.'s mom: well, I'd say Virginia'y problem would have been enough. It's too much of the same (story-wise!!! It's awful what happened to J.D.'s mom, and I'm the last person to talk sth. like that down!). It doesn't serve the story, really. Also, there is this thing the author made that feels to me like a big, big mistake: towards the end, J.D.'s dad comes to talk to Noah about something he hasn't told J.D. when he informed him about the reason his mother treats him as she does: he tells Noah a way more detailed (even more awful) version of what happened to J.D.'s mom, and the whole purpose I can see the author did this for is to make me, the reader, even more sympathetic for a mom who beats her son. Thing is: I <i>already</i> felt sorry for her; I'm actually capable of doing both: resenting her beating her innocent son <i>and</i> feeling sorry for her, astonishing, isn't it; so the gory detail felt not only unnecessary but unbelievable: what was J.D.'s dad's motivation to tell this to a stranger (Noah) <i>and</i> take the risk of Noah telling J.D. about it (a thing J.D.’s father says he doesn’t want)? From his words, it's so Noah doesn't think so bad of him and his wife, but really, that seems a bit far-stretched...<br /><br />So, as a conclusion, this book has great potential (characters, Noah’s reflections, his narrating), but Bart Yates tried to deal with too many irons in the fire and overdid things in the process, thus ruined it a bit for me. And, since this is a major story telling issue, I can’t—sadly—bring myself to give this book 4 stars, although I’d really want to. Argh! So conflicting.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8929702758588770490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer905138265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4892868187486045147">Part drama, part mystery, part LGBT fiction, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> is one of those books that really wants to be read in one day. As a reader, though, I wanted to savor it a bit longer, so I paced myself enough to take a couple more days to enjoy it. <br/><br/>And while Noah York, the main character and narrator of the novel, is brash, disrespectful, and incredibly snarky, I found myself liking him a lot and enjoying his commentary.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3630095038504431976">Re-Read during the Great Blackout of Aught Eight...<br/><br/>The fact that I re-read this should tell you how much I love it. I NEVER re-read fiction! This book has definitely found its way into my personal "Top 5."<br/><br/>Protagonist Noah tries to hold his life together and finds love--all while his family and home (both literally and figuratively) are crumbling around him. The character of Noah speaks and acts like a real teenager, and handles his newfound attraction to neighbor J.D. in much the same manner. No</span>
  <span id="freeText3630095038504431976" style="display:none">Re-Read during the Great Blackout of Aught Eight...<br /><br />The fact that I re-read this should tell you how much I love it. I NEVER re-read fiction! This book has definitely found its way into my personal "Top 5."<br /><br />Protagonist Noah tries to hold his life together and finds love--all while his family and home (both literally and figuratively) are crumbling around him. The character of Noah speaks and acts like a real teenager, and handles his newfound attraction to neighbor J.D. in much the same manner. No stylized, overly-precious hyper-aware teenage caricature here!<br /><br />A quote:<br />"...true intimacy is really just the run of the mill, day to day stuff that happens without thinking. Thousands of simple, meaningless, comfortable ways you can be close to someone, never dreaming how shitty you'll feel when you wake up one morning with all of it gone."</span>

<div id="review_121124858" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121124858" />
    <a title="YA Reads Book Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1764232-ya-reads-book-reviews"><img alt="YA Reads Book Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285429046p2/1764232.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121124858?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YA Reads Book Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YA Reads Book Reviews" href="/user/show/1764232-ya-reads-book-reviews">YA Reads Book Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121124858" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14030103131760390282">When Noah’s mother packed up their lives and moved them from Chicago to a rural town in New England, Noah never expected to find love, especially not with the boy from across the street. But J.D. isn’t gay. No sir. He’s got a girlfriend, and they’ve even done it and everything. But since when did that mean anything at all? Noah isn’t stupid, and he’s not so completely blinded by his feelings for J.D. that he can’t see how J.D. feels. Even if J.D. can’t, or wont.<br/><br/>It’s not so easy for J.D., though.</span>
  <span id="freeText14030103131760390282" style="display:none">When Noah’s mother packed up their lives and moved them from Chicago to a rural town in New England, Noah never expected to find love, especially not with the boy from across the street. But J.D. isn’t gay. No sir. He’s got a girlfriend, and they’ve even done it and everything. But since when did that mean anything at all? Noah isn’t stupid, and he’s not so completely blinded by his feelings for J.D. that he can’t see how J.D. feels. Even if J.D. can’t, or wont.<br /><br />It’s not so easy for J.D., though. At least Noah has a supportive mother who loves him regardless of his sexual orientation. J.D. isn’t so lucky. He has a hard time getting any kind of affection out of his mother at the best of times; imagine how it would be for him if he came out…<br /><br />How long can you deny your feelings for someone, though? J.D. soon discovers that just being Noah’s friend isn’t enough. One thing leads to another and suddenly the whole town seems to know all their deepest and darkest secrets. And in this particular small American town, homosexuality isn’t looked upon favourably. Noah and J.D. find themselves in all kinds of trouble.<br /><br />Noah’s voice is raw, fresh, and so very real. He tells it like it is: straight up, no BS. He has a real simplistic way of breaking things down, of looking at the world. If there were more Noah Yorks walking the Earth, society would be a better place for all. While Leave Myself Behind deals with some serious, heartbreaking issues, it manages to do so lightly (for lack of a better word). Noah’s sarcasm and his sense of humour shine a little bit of light on an otherwise devastatingly depressing story. I know Noah and J.D.’s story may ring true for some, but I hope that not too many queer readers can relate to the boys’ experiences in this book. No one should ever have to go through what J.D. and Noah did. Not ever.<br /><br />But this is more than just a coming out story. This is a story about family, truth, and love – all kinds of love. As J.D. so sadly learns, sometimes love just isn’t enough. Sometimes the world throws so much at you, that not even love can heal the deepest wounds.<br />Leave Myself Behind comes with a tissue box warning, folks. You’ll cry from laughter, but I’m certain you’ll cry from sadness too. A must read for everyone, everywhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14030103131760390282" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9777270001649286904">I loved this.<br/><br/>Noah York is an unforgettable character. He's got a very honest and witty way of looking at the world. Also, he's adorable. You can't help but love him from the get-go. <br/><br/>Noah's seventeen and living with his mom, they're trying to find a new balance after his father died unexpectedly. They've just re-located and are remodelling the old house they moved into single-handedly. But the house has a story to tell and secrets to reveal: mason jars with mistifying content pop up all over the </span>
  <span id="freeText9777270001649286904" style="display:none">I loved this.<br /><br />Noah York is an unforgettable character. He's got a very honest and witty way of looking at the world. Also, he's adorable. You can't help but love him from the get-go. <br /><br />Noah's seventeen and living with his mom, they're trying to find a new balance after his father died unexpectedly. They've just re-located and are remodelling the old house they moved into single-handedly. But the house has a story to tell and secrets to reveal: mason jars with mistifying content pop up all over the house and his mother becomes obsessed with finding out the truth about the previous owner. <br /><br />Meanwhile Noah has met J.D., the cute boy next door. They become fast friends, but soon Noah feels more than friendship for J.D., who has a girlfriend much to Noah's dismay. As the boys grow closer and become virtually inseparable, Noah finds out that his feelings do not go unrequited. A beautiful, heart-warming first love story enfolds that has the boys dealing with very serious issues such as severe homophobia and the mental break-down of Noah's mother. Their love only grows stronger because of it, which is both moving and endearing. <br /><br />The writing is fast-paced, original and utterly engaging. It's a page-turner that will win over your heart. It's much more than a coming-out novel and deserves to be read by a wide audience.</span>

<div id="review_1429073350" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429073350" />
    <a title="G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5487874-g"><img alt="G." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456934593p2/5487874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1429073350?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="G." class="user" itemprop="url" name="G." href="/user/show/5487874-g">G.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429073350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2927351155018384656">3.5/5<br/><br/><i>Leave Myself Behind</i> is my first Bart Yates, and up till around 60% I was convinced it was a four star read. <br/><br/>For such a short book, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> sure does try to be a lot of things: it’s a coming-of-age story, a coming out story, a first love story, a story about families, a (sort of) mystery, a story about grieving and loss. See what I mean? It’s not that it deals with those themes badly, it's that it sure does start to feel crowded because it's all mushed into 256 pages. <br/><br/>I liked the</span>
  <span id="freeText2927351155018384656" style="display:none">3.5/5<br /><br /><i>Leave Myself Behind</i> is my first Bart Yates, and up till around 60% I was convinced it was a four star read. <br /><br />For such a short book, <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> sure does try to be a lot of things: it’s a coming-of-age story, a coming out story, a first love story, a story about families, a (sort of) mystery, a story about grieving and loss. See what I mean? It’s not that it deals with those themes badly, it's that it sure does start to feel crowded because it's all mushed into 256 pages. <br /><br />I liked the book even after the halfway mark, because Noah, the teenage protagonist and narrator of <i>Leave Myself Behind</i>, is great. I really, really liked his voice. Don’t get me wrong, Noah can be a real asshole, the teenage moody kind. The thing that makes Noah compelling and likable is his self-awareness. He knows when he’s being a dipshit, and he does not pat himself on the back for his dipshittiness. Not only that, Noah’s a multi-faceted character—he feels real. It’s fair to say that for me Noah made the novel. <br /><br />If I had to be specific about my rating, the thematically overcrowded plot would get three stars, and Noah would get four.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2927351155018384656" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_738009823" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/738009823" />
    <a title="Tiffany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7571542-tiffany"><img alt="Tiffany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542787877p2/7571542.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/738009823?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tiffany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tiffany" href="/user/show/7571542-tiffany">Tiffany</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7571542-tiffany?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7571542-tiffany?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7571542-tiffany?shelf=glbt">glbt</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer738009823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4233534160626376520"><b>I LOVEDDDDDDD THIS ONE!</b><br/><br/>I never wanted this to end! This book is definitely one of my favorites now. I accidentally finished it — you know, when there's 20 pages of <i>Reader's Guide</i> at the end of a book and you flip the page and scream, "Nooooooooooooooooo!" because you thought you still had a little bit of a perfect book left? And then I re-read a few of my favorite parts because I really wished it hadn't ended.<br/><br/>Noah and his mom move into a old victorian fixer-upper in a small town in New Hampshire</span>
  <span id="freeText4233534160626376520" style="display:none"><b>I LOVEDDDDDDD THIS ONE!</b><br /><br />I never wanted this to end! This book is definitely one of my favorites now. I accidentally finished it — you know, when there's 20 pages of <i>Reader's Guide</i> at the end of a book and you flip the page and scream, "Nooooooooooooooooo!" because you thought you still had a little bit of a perfect book left? And then I re-read a few of my favorite parts because I really wished it hadn't ended.<br /><br />Noah and his mom move into a old victorian fixer-upper in a small town in New Hampshire. Noah Immediately meets JD, who is a small-town-overly-nice neighbor about Noah's age, when JD comes ringing their doorbell to say hello. While Noah, his mom, and JD (since he offers to help them) renovate their home, they discover a mason jar in the wall with a poem in it. Noah's mom, who is already a slightly unbalanced woman, becomes very intrigued by the item that was purposely placed in the wall of the house. After finding another mason jar in another wall of the home, Virginia (Noah's mom) becomes obsessive in an unhealthy way with figuring out the mystery of what happened in this home. Meanwhile Noah, who is spending all this time with his new friend JD, starts to develop more than friendly feelings towards his new friend — but they're both straight...right?<br /><br />Noah is dealing with the possibly that he may be gay, even though he had never thought about it before, and his mother is becoming unhinged. Major secrets lay hidden in the walls of their new home, and major secrets are about to unfold in all of these characters' lives as well. <br /><br />Noah is a an extremely funny, honest, and very REAL character. JD is about the sweetest character there is. Virginia, Noah's mother, is crazy and wonderful at the same time. All of the other characters were so very real, and I became very emotionally invested in this novel. JD's parents made me angry and sick, yet I also felt pity for his dad. Everyone, even smaller characters that I didn't mention, pulled some kind of strong emotion from me at some point of this story. <br /><br />It had <i>magic</i> (that I hadn't felt since I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542151940" rel="nofollow noopener">Jellicoe Road</a>) in the mystery of the hidden pieces in the walls and what had happened to these people, it was an extremely witty story, and it had the <b>GREAT</b> emotional depth of a character that finds his true self and falls in love.<br /><br />This book is GLBT, and it is descriptive at times. I felt it was honest and I loved every word. This is definitely a book I will re-read again sometime. I'm still sad that it's over. 100% LOVED LOVED LOVED every bit!<br /><br />This is a pretty short review for me, but I don't think there are many other ways that I can say that I loved it and that it was perfect — period.</span>

<div id="review_176410167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176410167" />
    <a title="K.Z. Snow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2135051-k-z-snow"><img alt="K.Z. Snow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442485288p2/2135051.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.Z. Snow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.Z. Snow" href="/user/show/2135051-k-z-snow">K.Z. Snow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176410167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2000351915595461814"><b>4.75</b> rounded up<br/><br/>Since this is the author's first book, I'm not surprised it isn't as tightly constructed and powerful as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/871060.The_Brothers_Bishop' title='The Brothers Bishop by Bart Yates' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Brothers Bishop</a>. Still, it's a lovely read. <br/><br/>I do wish more time had been spent on the central relationship. The dramas that surrounded it, although fascinating at first, became distracting, and ultimately didn't add much to my understanding of the main characters or to their understanding of themselves and each other.<br/><br/>Nevertheless, I'm eagerly looking forward to the author's</span>
  <span id="freeText2000351915595461814" style="display:none"><b>4.75</b> rounded up<br /><br />Since this is the author's first book, I'm not surprised it isn't as tightly constructed and powerful as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/871060.The_Brothers_Bishop" title="The Brothers Bishop by Bart Yates" rel="nofollow noopener">The Brothers Bishop</a>. Still, it's a lovely read. <br /><br />I do wish more time had been spent on the central relationship. The dramas that surrounded it, although fascinating at first, became distracting, and ultimately didn't add much to my understanding of the main characters or to their understanding of themselves and each other.<br /><br />Nevertheless, I'm eagerly looking forward to the author's future work. Yates's gay-themed fiction has become an auto-buy for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2000351915595461814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_88569722" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/88569722" />
    <a title="Mackenzie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3258910-mackenzie"><img alt="Mackenzie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316966445p2/3258910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88569722?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mackenzie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mackenzie" href="/user/show/3258910-mackenzie">Mackenzie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3258910-mackenzie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88569722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13380314556259324936">Yates' debut novel tells the story of a 17-year-old named Noah York, who moves into a small town in New Hampshire, discovers his sexuality and unravels a mystery that influences his mother's past. There are plenty of twists and turns in the plot - I'm afraid I can't really say much about it here because the whole story is complex and multi-layered, so you'll just have to take my word for this (unless you decide to read it yourself). <br/><br/>It's very captivating, though. It's one of those stories that o</span>
  <span id="freeText13380314556259324936" style="display:none">Yates' debut novel tells the story of a 17-year-old named Noah York, who moves into a small town in New Hampshire, discovers his sexuality and unravels a mystery that influences his mother's past. There are plenty of twists and turns in the plot - I'm afraid I can't really say much about it here because the whole story is complex and multi-layered, so you'll just have to take my word for this (unless you decide to read it yourself). <br /><br />It's very captivating, though. It's one of those stories that once you read it, you won't be able to stop. There's a surprisingly high element of mystery in it, which makes for one very morbid twist near the end, but the mystery will definitely keep you interested. There's also plenty of poetry included in it (the main character's mother is a poet after all) so there are many things that can keep the readers glued to the book.<br /><br />The story's in a first person point of view. I'm usually iffy about that, but Noah's voice as the narrator is very engaging. And when I say engaging, I really mean it. The main reason why a reader would get sucked into and stay with the story is because of Noah's 'voice'. He's the kind of character who will probably get on your nerves if you meet him in real life - I know he would grate on mine - but would be someone very interesting to meet and get to know. <br /><br />Noah's thoughts are disorganized so his narration can be completely disjointed. He would be talking about one thing (for example, the situation he was currently in) and segues out of nowhere to an account of his childhood with his dad. He would end this narration by saying, "I don't know why it seems important to tell you this". But that's not sloppy writing from Yates, in my opinion. In fact, that's what makes the book so interesting! It's a testament to how good Yates is at giving his character a believable personality. Noah is a teenager with a youthful mind who makes no pretense of being a genius. And while he may not be a genius, he's definitely not superficial either. He just <i>feels</i> REAL. That, I think, is the reason why I was able to become completely immersed in the story.<br /><br />On the other hand, the fact that Noah's thoughts and feelings are extremely significant - therefore, dominant - throughout the story might also be the reason for the one and only criticism I have for this book. His voice is so strong that the other characters come across as one-dimensional. The two major supporting characters are his mother Virginia and his boyfriend J. D., both of whom are central to his life, and the readers, of course, find out their stories and personalities through Noah as he talks at length about these two people, but despite their major roles, I feel that I don't really know anything about them in the end. I don't know what makes them tick - I can't even imagine such things. Their traits, physical appearances, hobbies and personalities are laid out in the story, but I still feel like they're strangers to me. Whereas Noah... he's become someone who's terribly familiar to me after finishing this book. <br /><br />I don't know whether this is intentional or not, but it does seem as if Yates, in making Noah a unique character, neglects to develop the others. This is admittedly not a fatal flaw - it's probably just me, as a reader, simply curious and wanting to know more about the other characters besides Noah.<br /><br /><i>Leave Myself Behind</i> seems to be a hard book to categorize. it features a gay youth, same-sex love scenes, and even issues related to the difficulties of coming out to society in the US. But is it gay fiction? Or is it mainstream fiction? That's the question many readers and reviewers, including critics, seem to ask. My opinion on this issue is this - for once, I think I'm going to go with mainstream fiction.<br /><br />Although the character is gay, the point of the story isn't about gay relationships. It's about family dynamics and the process of growing up. Even compared to <b>Andre Aciman</b>'s <b>Call My By Your Name</b>, a similar story about a young boy who is coming of age and discovering his sexuality, this one has a wider range of issues to deal with. It also isn't a love story, which is what <i>Call Me By Your Name</i> is. I've read enough gay novels to know if one IS NOT a gay novel. So, yes, I would say <i>Leave Myself Behind</i> should be categorized as mainstream fiction and marketed as thus.<br /><br />Since we're on the subject, if you ask me, which book is better - <i>Call Me By Your Name</i> or this one - I wouldn't be able to choose. I will settle for saying that these two books are like apples and oranges. They're very much different that any comparison beyond the sexuality of their lead male characters would seem ridiculous. Aciman's novel is adorable because of its poetic language, but Yates' writing is edgy and gritty. So it's a matter of taste - you pick one which one is more to your liking - but both books are equally good.<br /><br />Having said that, this book <i>does</i> get compared to another work of literature... something even 'bigger' than Aciman's work. It gets compared <u>a lot</u> to <b>J. D. Salinger</b>'s <b>Catcher in the Rye</b> and the character Noah to Holden Caufield. That, I think, is an astonishing achievement, simply because Salinger's work is so famous and is considered classic. Having never <i>Catcher...</i>, however, I can't really say how true this comparison is... all I can say is that it is perhaps worthy and well-deserved. This isn't a shallow novel, after all; it's a realistic coming-of-age story that is very well-written and enjoyable.<br /><br />As far as debut novel goes, this one deserves an applause. It's not a mistake this book won the Alex Award by the American Library Association, or any other awards it might have won... Bart Yates is definitely one author to look out for. He has two other books published as well, <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Brothers-Bishop-Bart-Yates/dp/0758209126/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Brothers Bishop</b>
</a> and <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Distance-Between-Us-Bart-Yates/dp/0758226977/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Distance Between us</b>
</a>. <br /><br />I've already read <i>Brothers Bishop</i>, which is a novel so full of angst and no happy ending that depressed me so much after I read it I felt suffocated. It still managed to mesmerize me, though - talk about <i>powerful writing</i>. That's why, despite the agonizing angst of Yates' second novel, I'm still planning to buy the third title from Yates. I've simply come to love his writing and style. <i>Leave Myself Behind</i>, however, remains the best out of the two I've read and I highly recommend this to anyone who's looking for a good book to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13380314556259324936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_837935304" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/837935304" />
    <a title="Christina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28162744-christina"><img alt="Christina" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/837935304?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina" href="/user/show/28162744-christina">Christina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer837935304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3873636827025440774">I loved this book. It was pure fate and luck that I happened upon it in my school library and decided to take it. It was hidden on a bottom shelf and with the light coloring of the cover I almost missed it. I think it was the title the cause me to grab it. Usually I'm not a big fan of the self discovery type of books but some how that day the title, Leave Myself Behind, appealed to me.<br/><br/>One word to describe this book would be raw. It is dirty, earthy, and grounded. Curses are sprinkled in it givin</span>
  <span id="freeText3873636827025440774" style="display:none">I loved this book. It was pure fate and luck that I happened upon it in my school library and decided to take it. It was hidden on a bottom shelf and with the light coloring of the cover I almost missed it. I think it was the title the cause me to grab it. Usually I'm not a big fan of the self discovery type of books but some how that day the title, Leave Myself Behind, appealed to me.<br /><br />One word to describe this book would be raw. It is dirty, earthy, and grounded. Curses are sprinkled in it giving it character and the educated quirkiness of the main character drew me in. How often does one find a main character in a fiction book that makes references to Neitzche and other various philosophers, thinkers, and writers. It is just so relate-able to the average high school student. So many high school romance books dumb down the characters to greatly wanting love and magically having great gpas and test scores; However, that intelligence shown in that magical gpa is never demonstrated fully in the character.<br /><br />The substance of this book was also very interesting. It was a romance, yet it wasn't completely targeted at the lovey-doviness of it all. It more of a journey with romance that just happened to be equally entwined in the plot. The romance in the novel was a homosexual one, but I don't think that part is needed to be emphasized. A good romance shouldn't matter whether the love is homosexual, heterosexual, etc. but by the fact that it was more of a process where it all happened naturally.<br /><br />I absolutely fell in love with the main character, Noah. I already remarked that he is very educated and relate-able to the average student. His education is shown very clearly in his character and his intelligence is very appealing. Noah's colorful language is just beautiful. I tell you, its very rare these days to find a teenager who doesn't curse at least mentally. His honesty in the expressal of his feelings is so straight forward the reader gets hit with his ever emotion and thought.<br /><br />Noah is also not a weak character; He has a pretty solid backbone. He knows love when he sees it, yet he won't let it rule him- to bend him. In the story after J.D. and Noah finally discover that yes, the love each other, they face the dilemma of J.D.'s girlfriend. J.D. wanted desperately to keep their relationship a secret and keep is girlfriend. Noah would have none of it. He very blatantly states: Yes, I do love you but you can't keep me in the shadows J.D.. Make your choice. This standing of his ground is just something that we rarely see these days in high school romances. High schoolers are usually portrayed as so desperate at seeking love they would stoop to any level. This standing gives a larger perspective the character of an average teenagers. We aren't all sad little people living for our moment of finding affection.<br /><br />The only iffy part of this book was the fact that everyone was raped: Noah's mother, J.D.'s mother, Nancy. Seriously? That is a lot of assault going on in one novel. I understand that that author was trying to get a point across to connect all the stories together but this felt like a little much. It definitely passed the line of coincidence to forced planning. There were also various little cliches lightly dusted around but I excused those. I like the fact that this book didn't solve all its writing problems. Its flaws added to its character and that's a rare thing for me to say.<br /><br />I also didn't see much point to the side story that involved the jars, and all the rapes. It was simply just there. Yes, it added to some of my fascination with this book but I didn't find it particularly connected to the main plot of the story.<br /><br />However, on a note of surprise, I came to find J.D.'s father to be one of my favorite characters also. He really didn't have a large part or even a point in the story but I frankly don't care. His simple drunken attitude plainly appealed to me. To me he showed that life wasn't always a wonderful adventure. Sometimes to will prove to turn out to be plain sucky, yet we still continue living the same repetitive life. He was real and I liked that.<br /><br />Yes, this book has its flaws, but somehow the flaws in this book just makes it more precious. The cleverness of Noah's character is enticing and the whole book is a picture of raw reality. I would definitely recommend this book as a must read.<br /><br />read more reviews on my blog: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://allthingsappealingandnice.blogspot.com/">http://allthingsappealingandnice.blog...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3873636827025440774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
