    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1793796086" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793796086" />
    <a title="David Avery" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57460943-david-avery"><img alt="David Avery" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473108525p2/57460943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793796086?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Avery" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Avery" href="/user/show/57460943-david-avery">David Avery</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793796086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18223627258085325250">I thought this was a very good, if imperfect book, and I recommend it to readers looking to read gay fiction written from a YA perspective. Despite a few flaws, I enjoyed it overall and looked forward to reading each installment.<br/><br/>It started off rather unevenly for me and didn’t seem authentic. The book is set in New Hampshire, but the place names – Oakland, Cassidy, and Cody – felt like names of the West, not New England (a region I know well), and it did not surprise me to learn that the author </span>
  <span id="freeText18223627258085325250" style="display:none">I thought this was a very good, if imperfect book, and I recommend it to readers looking to read gay fiction written from a YA perspective. Despite a few flaws, I enjoyed it overall and looked forward to reading each installment.<br /><br />It started off rather unevenly for me and didn’t seem authentic. The book is set in New Hampshire, but the place names – Oakland, Cassidy, and Cody – felt like names of the West, not New England (a region I know well), and it did not surprise me to learn that the author is from Wyoming. It also felt to me that the author was channeling J.D. Salinger a little too closely. The jaded, disaffected Noah felt like a gay Holden Caulfield to me living with Glass-like characters (quirky poet types with depression). Noah’s thoughts and voice also felt at first like the voice of an adult woman writing a gay character rather than the voice of a gay teenage boy.<br /><br />But, about 25% of the way in, everything started to come together. The voice suddenly felt more authentic to me as Noah set his eyes on new friend JD, and the story took off. There’s a lot of drama in this story, as other reviewers have mentioned – perhaps too much. An awful lot happens in a brief period in Noah’s life in a way that didn’t really ring true. But, life can be stranger than fiction sometimes and I don’t take points off for that. I enjoyed the growing relationship between Noah and JD, which felt real and tender. The oppression they faced felt real, too. I really didn't like the horrific events both Noah's and JD's mothers had in common, and I always find it lazy and offensive when authors use it as a plot point (particularly when used twice!). The last part of the book dropped off for me, and the ending felt rushed.<br /><br />Flaws notwithstanding, I liked this book overall and am glad I read it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18223627258085325250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kira" href="/user/show/39116645-kira">Kira</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1477183717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4976445165239457177">When in slump, read some good YA. It almost always works for me.<br/><br/>After an initial hiccup I was won over by brutal honesty of Noah, the narrator, enamored by him and J.D., the boy next door, falling in love with and helping each other to work through multiple problems with their parents (and they there real problems here, not "teenage world problems"). Even in YA it's rare to see that level of savoring and, for the lack of better word, beauty when it comes to feelings, without it being buried down</span>
  <span id="freeText4976445165239457177" style="display:none">When in slump, read some good YA. It almost always works for me.<br /><br />After an initial hiccup I was won over by brutal honesty of Noah, the narrator, enamored by him and J.D., the boy next door, falling in love with and helping each other to work through multiple problems with their parents (and they there real problems here, not "teenage world problems"). Even in YA it's rare to see that level of savoring and, for the lack of better word, beauty when it comes to feelings, without it being buried down in the physical side of a relationship. And for those who hate teenage whining and angst that often accompany YA novels, both Noah and J.D. were impressively mature.<br />I was touched by the way author showed Noah and his mother dealing with grief and chilled by the horrifying events adults here had to live through and all the complexities of its aftermath.<br /><br />A couple of things that made me give the book 4 stars: I couldn't buy characters doing and redoing renovations of the house that easily and that fast. I wish Yates put in at least a little more realism into that. Also, at some point Noah was coming across as annoyingly obtuse because some mysteries were too easy to crack for a reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4976445165239457177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3431673369236635428">My friend Leo recommended this to me, and I knew from the beginning that it wasn't going to be light and sweet all the way through. Both Noah and J.D. are great characters. Noah's wit and sarcasm made me laugh and cry. J.D. is a sweet boy who is punished for something he didn't do. I just wanted to take both boys and hug them tightly.<br/><br/>It was an extremely emotional read for me. The bullying and intolerance that Noah and J.D. experienced at school really hits home because I know those things happen</span>
  <span id="freeText3431673369236635428" style="display:none">My friend Leo recommended this to me, and I knew from the beginning that it wasn't going to be light and sweet all the way through. Both Noah and J.D. are great characters. Noah's wit and sarcasm made me laugh and cry. J.D. is a sweet boy who is punished for something he didn't do. I just wanted to take both boys and hug them tightly.<br /><br />It was an extremely emotional read for me. The bullying and intolerance that Noah and J.D. experienced at school really hits home because I know those things happen to my students daily, and there is nothing I can do about it when it doesn't happen in front of me and no one tells me about it.<br /><br />Both mothers, Virginia and Donna, reminded me a bit of my mom. Their actions brought up some painful memories and those memories plus the effect those women had on the two boys in the book brought me to tears more than once.<br /><br />All in all, this was a great book. Everything isn't tied up in a neat bow at the end, but I was satisfied with the ending the author provided.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3431673369236635428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText6018630180229856567" style="display:none">Well...I loved the first 60 percent, loved it! But then my belief began floating away. I know not one person on this planet who would just ignore someone the way Noah ignored his mother. The book fell a star and a half for that, and another half a star for the unimaginative beating. It seemed quite rushed, and pretty much tossed in there for affect. Pity really.<br /><br />I did love Noah, his brutal honesty about himself, his affection for his father, and the mystery they unraveled within their new home. I also applaud the author for reasoning out his intelligence, because this is one smart kid.<br /><br />And let me not forget J.D. Even though I puzzled out the reason behind his issues at home pages before the reveal, I enjoyed the author's cleverness within. And a high-five to his father...<br /><br />Would I recommmend this? Heck yeah! The writing is superb, the story engaging, and, well, Noah and J.D. are a not-so-perfect-but-oh-so-human couple. This book leaves you rooting for them long after you read the last word. </span>

  <span id="freeText14764093356170676362" style="display:none"> The life of seventeen year old Noah York is simple. He has an insane yet poetic mother, he lives in a new home where hidden mason jars hold disturbing secrets, and he is infatuated with the boy next door known as J.D.<br /> Ummm... ok... maybe it's not that simple.<br /> Noah goes through a hard moment in his life as he tries to figure out his love for D.J. and tries to hold the truth inside from his mother who is absolutely obsessed with the mason jars that lie in the walls of the new house. But lately, his mom has been acting very strange and has violent nightmares and wakes up even more weirder.<br /> J.D., who is suddenly confused about the love that Noah shows toward him, tries to untangle the knot in his mind while hiding extremely tormenting pain behind a simple smile. <div id="review_48380461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48380461" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48380461?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted" href="/user/show/686685-ted">Ted</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">16 and up</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/686685-ted?shelf=gay-fiction">gay-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48380461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7423004850067985718">"Leave Myself Behind" is a very poignant short novel. I read it in two sittings (which, for me, is practically unprecedented). Noah, the narrator is "a hilariously profane and searingly honest" 17yo boy who paints, plays the trombone, and who falls in love with another boy from school who lives across the street. But this is not YA palaver. Themes include poetry, family love, family secrets, nice guys vs. shitheads, and being forced to think as an adult at an early age. Yates' style is economica</span>
  <span id="freeText7423004850067985718" style="display:none">"Leave Myself Behind" is a very poignant short novel. I read it in two sittings (which, for me, is practically unprecedented). Noah, the narrator is "a hilariously profane and searingly honest" 17yo boy who paints, plays the trombone, and who falls in love with another boy from school who lives across the street. But this is not YA palaver. Themes include poetry, family love, family secrets, nice guys vs. shitheads, and being forced to think as an adult at an early age. Yates' style is economical and direct, as in his following novel "The Brothers Bishop." Noah's "voice" is that of an authentic 21st century teenager. Highly recommended.<br /><br />For some reviews that say it better than I can, check out <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bartyates.com/lmbreviews.htm">http://www.bartyates.com/lmbreviews.htm</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7423004850067985718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/587924489" />
    <a title="A" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10107035-a"><img alt="A" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351374771p2/10107035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/587924489?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A" href="/user/show/10107035-a">A</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10107035-a?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer587924489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1263573690489826967">Okay. This is my latest obsession. A friend sent it to me when I was complaining about my Trumble slump. (JH Trumble has ruined reading for me with her goodness.) Now I'm in a Yates slump, though I plan on moving on to his other book ASAP. It's more than a YA coming of age, though it does that extremely well. It's compelling and rich and linguistically beautiful and gives you that horrible feeling of anxiety in your stomach as you read because it feels like something terrible could happen at any</span>
  <span id="freeText1263573690489826967" style="display:none">Okay. This is my latest obsession. A friend sent it to me when I was complaining about my Trumble slump. (JH Trumble has ruined reading for me with her goodness.) Now I'm in a Yates slump, though I plan on moving on to his other book ASAP. It's more than a YA coming of age, though it does that extremely well. It's compelling and rich and linguistically beautiful and gives you that horrible feeling of anxiety in your stomach as you read because it feels like something terrible could happen at any time, but also something amazing could as well. At times horrific, at times hopeful, Yates is a literary talent, regardless of genre. Yes, it gets a bit heavy-handed at times, but the intensity is gorgeous. I loved it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1263573690489826967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/7NXTKE0HG+rBWkC3pv+tNbE/MQ+cbitAln7N7APaO+0MOXbyoZhuYhfvwxZdUCOulb3QfkJUddvL8acsugxFA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('66WgSHj0qTseBMDEssZ8DWgOdzKAGhrtlmwFzV9X4UWgJhLfE0bU7T1eFso1KMI3BJx8t0di85f7GjhmXbC4vg==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9548953970929165865">Author also writes as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7126915.Noah_Bly' title='Noah Bly' rel='nofollow noopener'>Noah Bly</a><br/><br/>The youngest of three brothers, Bart Yates was born in Cheyenne, Wyoming, in 1962,<br/>to Newell and Lois Yates. In 1969 his family moved to Lamoni, Iowa, where his father<br/>was the Dean of Students at Graceland College and his mother taught business courses, also at Graceland. Bart graduated from Lamoni High School in 1981, Drake University in 1985 (with a Bachelor of Music </span>
  <span id="freeText9548953970929165865" style="display:none">Author also writes as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7126915.Noah_Bly" title="Noah Bly" rel="nofollow noopener">Noah Bly</a><br /><br />The youngest of three brothers, Bart Yates was born in Cheyenne, Wyoming, in 1962,<br />to Newell and Lois Yates. In 1969 his family moved to Lamoni, Iowa, where his father<br />was the Dean of Students at Graceland College and his mother taught business courses, also at Graceland. Bart graduated from Lamoni High School in 1981, Drake University in 1985 (with a Bachelor of Music degree in Clarinet Performance), and Boston University in 1988 (with a Master of Music degree in Woodwind Performance).<br /><br />Following a brief. bizarrely inappropriate stint as bursar at The Boston Conservatory, Bart began teaching private music lessons in Massachusetts in 1989, and then moved to Iowa City, Iowa, in 1999. A novel-writing class taught by Gordon Mennenga at the University of Iowa Summer Writing Festival was the catalyst for Bart’s first novel (Leave Myself Behind), which was published in 2003 by Kensington Books, and won an Alex Award from the American Library Association in 2004. His second novel, The Brothers Bishop, was published in 2006, and his third, The Distance Between Us, in 2009. His most recent novel, The Third Hill North Of Town, came out in 2014, under the pen name of Noah Bly.<br /><br />Bart still teaches clarinet, saxophone, and bass guitar lessons in Iowa City and Cedar Rapids, and has also taught workshops at the University of Iowa's Summer Writing Festival. When not teaching or writing, Bart can usually be found with a book in one hand and a glass of red wine in the other---or coffee, as often as not.<br /><br />Bart is currently working on his fifth novel.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9548953970929165865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
